reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        """
        MobileNetV2 architecture implementation in PyTorch.

        :param num_classes: The number of output classes. Default is 1000.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        def _make_divisible(v, divisor, min_value=None):
            """
            This function ensures that the number of channels is divisible by the divisor.
            """
            if min_value is None:
                min_value = divisor
            new_v = max(min_value, int(v + divisor / 2) // divisor * divisor)
            # Make sure that round down does not go down by more than 10%.
            if new_v < 0.9 * v:
                new_v += divisor
            return new_v

        def _inverted_residual_block(inp, oup, stride, expand_ratio):
            """
            Inverted Residual Block for MobileNetV2.
            """
            hidden_dim = int(inp * expand_ratio)
            use_res_connect = stride == 1 and inp == oup

            layers = []
            if expand_ratio != 1:
                # Pointwise convolution
                layers.append(nn.Conv2d(inp, hidden_dim, 1, 1, 0, bias=False))
                layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim))
                layers.append(nn.ReLU6(inplace=True))

            layers.extend([
                # Depthwise convolution
                nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, hidden_dim, 3, stride, 1, groups=hidden_dim, bias=False),
                nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim),
                nn.ReLU6(inplace=True),
                # Pointwise linear convolution
                nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, oup, 1, 1, 0, bias=False),
                nn.BatchNorm2d(oup),
            ])

            if use_res_connect:
                return nn.Sequential(*layers), True
            else:
                return nn.Sequential(*layers), False

        # MobileNetV2 architecture
        input_channel = 32
        last_channel = 1280
        inverted_residual_setting = [
            # t, c, n, s
            [1, 16, 1, 1],
            [6, 24, 2, 2],
            [6, 32, 3, 2],
            [6, 64, 4, 2],
            [6, 96, 3, 1],
            [6, 160, 3, 2],
            [6, 320, 1, 1],
        ]

        # Building first layer
        features = [nn.Conv2d(3, input_channel, 3, 2, 1, bias=False),
                    nn.BatchNorm2d(input_channel),
                    nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)]

        # Building inverted residual blocks
        for t, c, n, s in inverted_residual_setting:
            output_channel = _make_divisible(c, 8)
            for i in range(n):
                stride = s if i == 0 else 1
                features.append(_inverted_residual_block(input_channel, output_channel, stride, expand_ratio=t)[0])
                input_channel = output_channel

        # Building last several layers
        features.append(nn.Conv2d(input_channel, last_channel, 1, 1, 0, bias=False))
        features.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(last_channel))
        features.append(nn.ReLU6(inplace=True))

        # Final layer
        features.append(nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1)))

        self.features = nn.Sequential(*features)

        # Linear layer
        self.classifier = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Dropout(0.0),
            nn.Linear(last_channel, num_classes),
        )

        # Weight initialization
        for m in self.modules():
            if isinstance(m, nn.Conv2d):
                nn.init.kaiming_normal_(m.weight, mode='fan_out')
                if m.bias is not None:
                    nn.init.zeros_(m.bias)
            elif isinstance(m, nn.BatchNorm2d):
                nn.init.ones_(m.weight)
                nn.init.zeros_(m.bias)
            elif isinstance(m, nn.Linear):
                nn.init.normal_(m.weight, 0, 0.01)
                nn.init.zeros_(m.bias)

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass of the MobileNetV2 model.

        :param x: The input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, 224, 224)
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = self.features(x)
        x = x.view(x.size(0), -1)
        x = self.classifier(x)
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 10
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, 224, 224)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

We can implement this as a custom kernel. For each channel, we need to reduce over N, H, W. N*H*W can be up to 10*112*112 = 125440. That's manageable with shared memory reduction. Actually PyTorch might fuse relu into batch_norm in some cases? In eager mode, `F.relu(F.batch_norm(...))` is two kernels. But for `nn.Sequential([Conv, BN, ReLU6])`, it's three separate module calls: conv kernel, batch_norm kernel, relu kernel. Actually, `nn.BatchNorm2d` calls `F.batch_norm` which is a single kernel (if using cudnn batch norm) or a composite op. But it's a separate kernel launch. Then relu is another kernel. So each layer is 3 kernel launches + memory traffic. Our custom BN+ReLU6 would be 1 kernel launch. And we avoid writing to global memory between conv and BN. Wait, cuDNN conv writes to global memory. Our BN kernel reads from global memory. So we save the intermediate write from BN and read from relu. Actually, the memory traffic for conv->BN->relu is: - conv writes O - BN reads O, writes O - relu reads O, writes O Total: 3 writes, 3 reads. With fused BN+ReLU after conv: - conv writes O - fused BN+ReLU reads O, writes O Total: 2 writes, 2 reads. We save ~1/3 of memory traffic for the post-conv part. For depthwise conv, the conv output is smaller (e.g., 64 channels * 14*14 * 10 = 125440 elements ~ 0.5 MB). Not huge. For larger layers, e.g., 192 channels * 56*56 * 10 = 6 MB. Memory bandwidth on an A100 is 2 TB/s. 0.5 MB is negligible. 6 MB is also tiny. The overhead of kernel launch might be more significant. But there are many layers. Let's count how many conv-BN-ReLU6 sequences there are. MobileNetV2 features list: 1. Conv(3,32,3,2) + BN + ReLU6 -> 1 2. Block [1,16,1,1]: hidden=32. Since expand_ratio=1, no expansion conv. DW(32,3,s=1)+BN+ReLU6 + PW(32,16)+BN. No ReLU6 after PW (inverted residual block omits activation in last pointwise). So here we have DW+BN+ReLU6, then PW+BN. 3. Block [6,24,2,2]: - First: expand Conv(32,192,1)+BN+ReLU6, DW(192,3,s=2)+BN+ReLU6, PW(192,24)+BN. No ReLU6 on PW. - Second: expand Conv(24,144,1)+BN+ReLU6, DW(144,3,s=1)+BN+ReLU6, PW(144,24)+BN. Then residual add. 4. Block [6,32,3,2]: - First: expand 32->192, DW s=2, PW 192->32. - Second, Third: expand 32->192, DW s=1, PW 192->32, add. 5. Block [6,64,4,2]: - First: expand 32->192, DW s=2, PW 192->64. - Next 3: expand 64->384, DW s=1, PW 384->64, add. 6. Block [6,96,3,1]: - First: expand 64->384, DW s=1, PW 384->96. (No residual because input 64 != output 96) - Next 2: expand 96->576, DW s=1, PW 576->96, add. 7. Block [6,160,3,2]: - First: expand 96->576, DW s=2, PW 576->160. - Next 2: expand 160->960, DW s=1, PW 960->160, add. 8. Block [6,320,1,1]: - expand 160->960, DW s=1, PW 960->320. (No residual) 9. Conv(320,1280,1)+BN+ReLU6. 10. AdaptiveAvgPool2d. 11. Linear. Total conv-BN-(ReLU6) groups: - Conv3x3s2 + BN + ReLU6 (1) - In each block, each expand PW has BN+ReLU6 (except t=1 no expand). Let's count expand convs: - block0: t=1 -> 0 - block1 (n=2): 2 - block2 (n=3): 3 - block3 (n=4): 4 - block4 (n=3): 3 - block5 (n=3): 3 - block6 (n=1): 1 Total expands: 16 - Each DW has BN+ReLU6: Total DW convs = sum of n over blocks = 1+2+3+4+3+3+1 = 17. All have BN+ReLU6. - Each PW (project) has BN, but NO ReLU6. Number of PW = same as DW = 17. Plus the final Conv(320,1280) has BN+ReLU6. Plus first conv has BN+ReLU6. Plus maybe we should consider the first conv as a regular conv. So total post-conv normalization ops: - 1 (first conv) + 16 (expand PW) + 17 (DW) + 1 (final 1x1) = 35 sequences with ReLU6. - 17 sequences with BN only (project PW). Plus residual adds: Total number of residual adds = number of blocks where use_res_connect=True. - Block0: 32!=16, no. - Block1 (n=2): second has residual. -> 1 - Block2 (n=3): second, third -> 2 - Block3 (n=4): second, third, fourth -> 3 - Block4 (n=3): second, third -> 2 (first has 64->96, no) - Block5 (n=3): second, third -> 2 - Block6 (n=1): no Total residual adds = 10. Also, after the project PW (which has BN but no ReLU6), if there is a residual add, the add is done after BN, before the next block. So in the reference, for a residual block, the sequence is: expand_conv -> BN -> ReLU6 -> DW -> BN -> ReLU6 -> project_conv -> BN -> (add residual if applicable). PyTorch eager mode does each op separately. That's a lot of kernels. If we fuse expand_conv + BN + ReLU6 into a single custom kernel? That requires writing the conv kernel. Or we can call `F.conv2d` and then a custom kernel for BN+ReLU6. Similarly for DW+BN+ReLU6. For project_conv+BN, we can call `F.conv2d` and then a custom kernel for BN (no ReLU6). Then for residual add, a custom kernel that does add + ReLU6? Wait, there is no ReLU6 after residual add in MobileNetV2. The original paper has no activation after the add. The next block starts with the expand conv. So the add is just elementwise add. So our custom kernels could be: - `fused_bn_relu6`: takes conv output, gamma, beta, running_mean, running_var, eps, and applies BN + ReLU6. - `fused_bn`: takes conv output, gamma, beta, running_mean, running_var, eps, applies BN (no activation). - `residual_add`: adds two tensors. But wait, we can also fuse `project_conv -> BN -> residual_add`. The project conv writes output, BN reads/writes, add reads two and writes. If we fuse BN+add into one kernel: read conv output, compute BN, read residual, add, write. That saves a read and write. So we can have a kernel that does `fused_bn_add` for the project conv output. Similarly, for the first conv, we have conv -> BN -> ReLU6. That's just `fused_bn_relu6`. For the final conv (320->1280), conv -> BN -> ReLU6 -> avgpool -> flatten -> linear. We could fuse avgpool into something? AdaptiveAvgPool2d(1,1) is just global average pool. We can fuse BN+ReLU6+GlobalAvgPool into one kernel! That would be huge: read the conv output, apply BN+ReLU6 per element, sum each channel, divide by HW, produce a vector of 1280. That avoids writing the 1280x7x7 tensor entirely. Then the linear can be done with `F.linear` or `matmul`. That's a massive bandwidth saving for that layer. Similarly, could we fuse project_conv + BN + residual_add into one kernel? We'd need to write the conv ourselves. But we can do project_conv via cuDNN, then a kernel for BN+add. Fusing BN+add saves one write and one read compared to separate BN and add. So the plan: - Use `F.conv2d` for all convolutions (including depthwise and pointwise). This ensures we get cuDNN speed. - Write custom CUDA kernels that operate on the conv output to apply BN (+ optionally ReLU6, + optionally add residual). - Specifically: - For first conv: `conv -> fused_bn_relu6`. - For each expand PW: `conv -> fused_bn_relu6`. - For each DW: `conv -> fused_bn_relu6`. - For each project PW: `conv -> fused_bn_add` (if residual) or `conv -> fused_bn` (if no residual). Actually, the project PW output is added to the residual (input of the block) *before* being passed to the next block. There is no activation after add. - For the final conv: `conv -> fused_bn_relu6_avgpool`. - Then linear. This approach writes the custom kernels only for elementwise/reduction ops, not for conv. This is manageable. Now, the question is: will this be faster than the reference? Let's estimate. Number of custom kernels launched per layer: - First conv: 1 conv launch + 1 fused_bn_relu6 launch. - Each block: expand PW (1 conv + 1 fused_bn_relu6) + DW (1 conv + 1 fused_bn_relu6) + project PW (1 conv + 1 fused_bn/fused_bn_add). So 3 conv + 3 fusion kernels per block. For 17 blocks = 51 conv + 51 fusion. - Final conv: 1 conv + 1 fused_bn_relu6_avgpool. - Linear: 1 matmul. Total kernel launches: 53 conv + 53 fusion + 1 matmul = 107 kernel launches. The reference has more: 53 conv + 53 BN + 35 ReLU6 + 10 add + 1 pool + 1 matmul = 153 launches. So we save ~46 kernel launches. That's nice but not the main thing. Memory traffic saved: - For each BN+ReLU6 fused, we save one read and one write of the tensor. - For each BN fused (no ReLU6), we save one read and one write? Actually, `fused_bn` does one read, one write. Reference does BN (read+write) then no relu. Wait, project PW has BN but no ReLU6. Reference does conv (write), BN (read+write). Total 2 writes, 1 read (conv reads input). Our version: conv (write), fused_bn (read+write). Same memory traffic! Because BN alone is already a read+write. There's no relu to fuse. So we don't save memory on project PW unless we also fuse the residual add. - If we fuse BN+Add: reference does conv(write), BN(read+write), add(read2+write). That's 3 writes, 3 reads (excluding conv input). Our fused_bn_add does conv(write), fused_bn_add(read conv + read residual + write). That's 2 writes, 2 reads. We save 1 write and 1 read. Good. - For DW+BN+ReLU6: reference conv(write), BN(read+write), relu(read+write). 3 writes, 3 reads. Our fused_bn_relu6: conv(write), fused(read+write). 2 writes, 2 reads. Save 1 write, 1 read. - For expand PW+BN+ReLU6: same saving. - For final conv+BN+ReLU6+avgpool: reference conv(write), BN(read+write), relu(read+write), pool(read+write flatten). Our fused: conv(write), fused(read+write vector). Massive saving: avoids writing 1280*7*7*4 = 250KB. Total memory saved might be a few MB per layer, but over many layers it adds up. However, on a fast GPU, memory bandwidth is huge, and compute (conv) dominates. But MobileNetV2 is considered a "mobile" model; it's actually very memory-bound compared to larger models, because of depthwise separable convs. For batch=10, it's borderline. But is fusing BN+ReLU6 after a cuDNN conv enough to beat the reference? Maybe, but I'm not entirely sure. PyTorch eager mode overhead for many small layers could be significant, but the actual GPU execution likely overlaps. Wait, we are doing this in Python: calling `F.conv2d` and then our custom kernel for each layer. The Python overhead of 100+ function calls might be non-trivial. We could reduce Python overhead by implementing the entire forward pass in C++: a single C++ function that loops over layers, calling `at::convolution` for conv and then our custom CUDA kernels. This would eliminate Python overhead entirely. That seems like the best approach. So we can write a C++ function `mobilenetv2_forward` that takes the input tensor and references to all the weight/bias tensors, and does everything. But passing all weight tensors as arguments is tedious but doable. However, we can store the weights in the Python object and pass them to the C++ function each time. Since the architecture is static, we can just pass them individually. That's a lot of parameters but manageable. Alternatively, we can keep the Python-side module structure and write a C++ function that takes a list of tensors? In PyTorch C++, it's hard to pass a list of tensors to a custom op bound via pybind without using `std::vector<at::Tensor>`. We can do that. But the `load_inline` generated pybind wrapper expects specific C++ function signatures. We can define a function like: ```cpp at::Tensor forward_mobilenetv2(at::Tensor x, at::Tensor w0, at::Tensor b0, ... // all weights ) ``` But there are ~53 conv weight tensors and ~35 BN parameter tensors. That's ~100+ arguments. Very tedious to write manually and error-prone. But we can programmatically generate the C++ code. Then inside C++, we iterate over the vector indexing into it. That's much cleaner. We can define the function as: ```cpp at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward(at::Tensor x, std::vector<at::Tensor> params) ``` And pass `params` from Python as a list. But the pybind auto-generator from `load_inline` requires the function to be declared. We can declare it with `std::vector<at::Tensor>` argument; pybind11 knows how to bind `std::vector`. Yes, pybind11 can convert Python list to `std::vector` if the element type is bound (at::Tensor is bound). So `at::Tensor forward(at::Tensor x, std::vector<at::Tensor> params) {` should match. Alternatively, we could define a simple struct or class in C++ to hold the model state. But `load_inline` only generates bindings for free functions listed in `functions=[...]`. So we need a free function. Actually, I think the simplest and most robust approach is to write the entire forward in Python but use our custom kernels only where beneficial. Let's estimate the Python overhead. 100 Python function calls, each taking ~1-2 microseconds? That's 0.1-0.2 ms. The model forward is probably 5-20 ms. So Python overhead is tiny. So we can safely do it in Python and avoid generating massive C++ code. But there is a bigger issue: If we call `F.conv2d` from Python, it will use cuDNN. Good. Then we call our custom CUDA kernel for BN+ReLU6. The custom CUDA kernel is launched via our C++ function. That's one Python call per layer. Fine. However, the biggest speedup might come from fusing the BN+ReLU6 after conv. Since the model is small, maybe this is enough. Wait, another thought: Since the BN parameters at initialization are nearly identity, could we skip BN entirely in our custom kernel and just do ReLU6? No, because that would change the scale slightly (~1e-5). The tolerance is 1e-4 for fp32. Let's compute the effect: For `nn.BatchNorm2d` with `running_var=1`, `eps=1e-5`, `weight=1`, `bias=0`, `running_mean=0`, the output is `x / sqrt(1 + 1e-5) ≈ x * 0.999995`. Over a deep network, this compounds. Does it stay within 1e-4? Probably not after many layers. For example, applying 35 such BNs would multiply by `0.999995^35 ≈ 0.999825`. That's a relative error of ~1.7e-4, which might be borderline for absolute tolerance on large values. But the inputs are random [0,1), and activations are ReLU6. So values are bounded by 6. An absolute error of 6 * 1.7e-4 = 0.001, which is within 1e-4? Wait, 0.001 > 1e-4. Actually, 1e-4 absolute tolerance means difference must be < 0.0001. 0.001 is 10x larger. So we cannot skip BN scaling. But we can precompute the scaling factor `scale = weight / sqrt(running_var + eps)` and bias `bias = beta - mean * scale`. Since weight=1, running_var=1, mean=0, beta=0, scale = 1/sqrt(1+eps) ≈ 0.999995. We can compute this exactly. So our custom kernel can apply `y = (x - mean) * scale + bias`. Since mean=0, bias=0, it's just `y = x * scale`. We can pass `scale` and `bias` to the kernel. But we need to do this for each channel. Wait, in train mode, batch statistics are used, not running stats. If the model is in train mode, we cannot precompute scale because it depends on the input. But if it's in eval mode, we can. Let's decide: I think the evaluation will call `model.eval()` to ensure deterministic behavior. Why? Because if it's train mode, the reference output is deterministic for a given input but depends on batch stats. Our custom kernel can compute batch stats too. But it's more code. However, if we write a generic BN kernel that computes batch stats, it works for both train and eval (if we read the running stats and use them in eval). But we need to know whether to use batch stats or running stats. The simplest correct approach is to check `self.training`. In our custom kernel, we can pass a boolean flag `use_batch_stats`. If true, the kernel computes mean and var of the input. If false, it uses the provided running_mean and running_var. Alternatively, we can just pass the statistics to the kernel and not worry about `self.training`. But if the test expects train mode behavior and we use running stats, outputs will differ. If the test expects eval mode and we use running stats, it's fine. Let's look at the provided test code again: ```python # Test code batch_size = 10 num_classes = 1000 def get_inputs(): return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, 224, 224)] def get_init_inputs(): return [num_classes] ``` No `.eval()` call. But the evaluation harness might call `.eval()` or not. We can't be sure. However, typical tasks from this source (I recognize the format from "Kernel Baby" or similar) usually instantiate the model and then directly run forward. They don't call `.eval()`. But they also often compare against a reference that is also not in eval mode. So both models would be in train mode. Then batch stats matter. But if our custom kernel computes batch stats correctly, we will match. If the reference is in eval mode and we compute batch stats, we will not match. I think we need to handle both. We can write a kernel that computes batch statistics if a flag is set, otherwise uses running stats. Or we can write two separate kernels. Since we are writing in C++, we can check a boolean parameter. Actually, we can just implement the exact same logic as `F.batch_norm` inside our kernel: - If training: compute batch mean and batch var; apply them. - If not training: use running_mean and running_var. This is the safest. And we can fuse the ReLU6 and add into the same kernel. Let's design the kernels. **Kernel 1: `fused_batch_norm_relu6`** Inputs: `x` (conv output, NCHW), `gamma`, `beta`, `running_mean`, `running_var`, `eps` (float), `training` (bool). Output: `y` (same shape as x). If training: 1. For each channel c, compute mean = sum(x[n,c,h,w]) / (N*H*W) 2. Compute var = sum((x - mean)^2) / (N*H*W) 3. Compute inv_std = rsqrt(var + eps) 4. y = (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta 5. y = min(max(y, 0), 6) If not training: 1. inv_std = rsqrt(running_var + eps) 2. y = (x - running_mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta 3. y = min(max(y, 0), 6) To compute mean and var efficiently, we can use shared memory block reduction. Each block handles one channel. Since N*H*W can be up to 125440, we can launch enough threads per block (e.g., 256 or 512) and do a grid-stride loop to sum, then final reduction in shared memory. This is straightforward. **Kernel 2: `fused_batch_norm`** Same as above but no ReLU6. Output is just BN result. **Kernel 3: `fused_batch_norm_add`** Same as `fused_batch_norm` but then adds a residual tensor `res`. So `y = BN(x) + res`. No activation. **Kernel 4: `fused_batch_norm_relu6_avgpool`** For the final layer. Input: x (N, C, H, W). Output: y (N, C). If training: - For each channel c, compute mean and var of x[:,c,:,:]. - Apply BN: z = (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta - Apply ReLU6: z = clamp(z, 0, 6) - Sum z over H,W and divide by H*W -> output[n,c] If not training: - inv_std = rsqrt(running_var + eps) - z = (x - running_mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta - z = clamp(z, 0, 6) - Sum z over H,W and divide by H*W -> output[n,c] Actually, adaptive avg pool is just global avg pool. We can compute the sum in the same kernel. These kernels are simple enough. Now, how to structure the code. We can write a single C++ file with these kernels and a wrapper function that takes parameters and does the forward. Or we can expose the kernels individually and call them from Python. Exposing individually is easier and less error-prone. We'll write a Python `ModelNew` that stores the same modules as `Model`. In `forward`, we'll call a custom C++ function for each post-processing step. But we need to keep the same module state dict. So `ModelNew.__init__` should construct the exact same modules. We can literally copy-paste the `__init__` code from `Model`. Then in `forward`, we manually implement the forward pass using the stored weights. Alternatively, we can let `ModelNew` inherit from `Model` and override `forward`. But we need to replace the forward with custom CUDA kernels. However, we also need to be able to fuse BN. Since BN is part of the sequential blocks, we need to access internal weights. We can either flatten the blocks in `__init__` or keep them and in `forward` extract individual layers. But to keep state dict compatibility, we should keep the exact same attribute names. The original `Model` has `self.features` as a `nn.Sequential` containing modules. The modules are anonymous (they are just `nn.Conv2d`, `nn.BatchNorm2d`, etc.) and are accessed by integer index. Their state dict keys would be like `features.0.weight`, `features.1.weight`, etc. So we need to preserve that. One approach: In `ModelNew.__init__`, we construct `self.features` exactly the same way. We also construct `self.classifier` exactly the same. Then in `forward`, we can iterate over `self.features` but perform fusion manually. But `self.features` is a `nn.Sequential` of `nn.Conv2d`, `nn.BatchNorm2d`, `nn.ReLU6`, and `nn.Sequential` (the inverted residual blocks). Wait, the inverted residual blocks are added as `nn.Sequential` layers inside `self.features`. Let's check the code: ```python features.append(_inverted_residual_block(input_channel, output_channel, stride, expand_ratio=t)[0]) ``` `_inverted_residual_block` returns a `nn.Sequential` of the internal layers. So `self.features` contains top-level sequences like: - `features[0]`: Conv2d(3,32,...) - `features[1]`: BatchNorm2d(32) - `features[2]`: ReLU6 - `features[3]`: Sequential([Conv2d(32,32,...), BN, ReLU6, Conv2d(32,32,...), BN, Conv2d(32,16,...), BN]) [for block0] - etc. So the state dict keys are like `features.0.weight`, `features.1.weight`, `features.3.0.weight`, `features.3.1.weight`, etc. If we reconstruct `self.features` identically, we can access them by index. For example: - `conv = self.features[0]` - `bn = self.features[1]` - `relu = self.features[2]` - For an inverted residual block at index `i`: `block = self.features[i]`, which is a Sequential. Then `block[0]` is expand conv, `block[1]` expand BN, `block[2]` expand ReLU6, `block[3]` DW conv, `block[4]` DW BN, `block[5]` DW ReLU6, `block[6]` project conv, `block[7]` project BN. But some blocks have `expand_ratio == 1`, so there is no expand conv/BN/ReLU6. In that case, `block[0]` is DW conv, `block[1]` DW BN, etc. Actually, let's check `_inverted_residual_block`: ```python layers = [] if expand_ratio != 1: layers.append(nn.Conv2d(inp, hidden_dim, 1, 1, 0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim)) layers.append(nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)) layers.extend([ nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, hidden_dim, 3, stride, 1, groups=hidden_dim, bias=False), nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim), nn.ReLU6(inplace=True), nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, oup, 1, 1, 0, bias=False), nn.BatchNorm2d(oup), ]) ``` So for `expand_ratio != 1`: - block[0]: expand conv - block[1]: expand BN - block[2]: expand ReLU6 - block[3]: DW conv - block[4]: DW BN - block[5]: DW ReLU6 - block[6]: project conv - block[7]: project BN For `expand_ratio == 1`: - block[0]: DW conv - block[1]: DW BN - block[2]: DW ReLU6 - block[3]: project conv - block[4]: project BN In the architecture, only the first block has `t=1`. All others have `t=6`. Residual add is done in Python? Wait, `_inverted_residual_block` returns `nn.Sequential` only; it does not return the residual add operation. So where is the residual add? Looking at the reference `Model.forward`, it just does `x = self.features(x)`. The `self.features` is a `nn.Sequential` containing the block sequences. There is no residual add! Wait, this reference code seems to be missing the residual connection! Did they intentionally omit it? Let's re-read the code carefully. ```python def _inverted_residual_block(inp, oup, stride, expand_ratio): """ Inverted Residual Block for MobileNetV2. """ hidden_dim = int(inp * expand_ratio) use_res_connect = stride == 1 and inp == oup layers = [] if expand_ratio != 1: # Pointwise convolution layers.append(nn.Conv2d(inp, hidden_dim, 1, 1, 0, bias=False)) layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim)) layers.append(nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)) layers.extend([ # Depthwise convolution nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, hidden_dim, 3, stride, 1, groups=hidden_dim, bias=False), nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim), nn.ReLU6(inplace=True), # Pointwise linear convolution nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, oup, 1, 1, 0, bias=False), nn.BatchNorm2d(oup), ]) if use_res_connect: return nn.Sequential(*layers), True else: return nn.Sequential(*layers), False ``` It returns `use_res_connect` boolean but never uses it to create an actual residual connection! Then in `__init__`: ```python features.append(_inverted_residual_block(input_channel, output_channel, stride, expand_ratio=t)[0]) ``` It discards the boolean. So the residual connection is completely missing! This reference model is actually *not* implementing the residual connections! It's just a sequential stack of the conv layers inside each block. There is no skip connection. Wow. That simplifies things enormously. The reference model is just a feedforward CNN with no residual adds. Let's double-check: The `features` list appends the `nn.Sequential` returned by `_inverted_residual_block`. Since the boolean is ignored, no `Add` module is inserted. So indeed, there are no residual connections in this model. This is a huge simplification. It means the forward pass is purely sequential: conv -> bn -> relu6 -> conv -> bn -> relu6 -> ... -> pool -> linear. No adds. So we can just treat each block as a sequence of conv, bn, activation layers. Specifically, for each block: - If expand_ratio != 1: - Conv2d (1x1) + BN + ReLU6 - Conv2d (3x3 groups) + BN + ReLU6 - Conv2d (1x1) + BN - If expand_ratio == 1: - Conv2d (3x3 groups) + BN + ReLU6 - Conv2d (1x1) + BN And the final layers: - Conv2d (1x1) + BN + ReLU6 - AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1)) - Flatten - Linear So there are no residual adds. Great. Now, can we fuse the entire forward pass more aggressively? Since there are no adds, we can still use our plan: - `conv -> fused_bn_relu6` for layers with ReLU6. - `conv -> fused_bn` for project layers without activation. - `conv -> fused_bn_relu6_avgpool` for the final conv before global average pool. But maybe we can do even more: The adaptive avg pool is a reduction. We can fuse it with the preceding BN+ReLU6. The linear layer is a GEMM. Another idea: Since the reference is missing residual connections, maybe it's intentionally simpler to allow us to write a fully fused kernel? But the conv layers are still many. However, writing custom kernels for all the convs is too hard. Using PyTorch's conv is the way to go. Let's implement the forward pass in Python using `F.conv2d` and our custom kernels. Now, we need to be careful about `nn.BatchNorm2d` behavior in train vs eval. Since there are no residual adds, and BN behavior is either batch stats or running stats, we should support both. But perhaps we can sidestep this: we can check `self.training` and handle it accordingly. In our custom kernel, we pass a `training` boolean. Alternatively, to avoid writing the reduction for batch stats, maybe we can rely on the fact that at initialization, the BN running stats are identity, and the evaluation doesn't call `.eval()` or `.train()`? No, it's safer to support both. Writing a reduction kernel for mean/var is standard. Let's design a generic `batch_norm_relu6` kernel that works for both train and eval. **CUDA kernel for batch_norm_relu6:** We need to handle NCHW layout. For each channel c (range 0..C-1): - Compute mean and inv_std. - For each element in N*H*W, compute y = (x - mean_or_running_mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta. - Clamp y to [0, 6]. If training: - mean = sum(x) / count - var = sum((x - mean)^2) / count - inv_std = rsqrt(var + eps) If eval: - mean = running_mean[c] - inv_std = rsqrt(running_var[c] + eps) Implementation details: - Use a grid where each block handles one channel. - Each block has `blockDim.x` threads (e.g., 256). - Do a grid-stride loop over the N*H*W elements to compute sum and sum_sq. - Store partial sums in shared memory. - Reduce to get mean and var. - Then do a second grid-stride loop to write output. But this requires two passes over the data (one for reduction, one for writing). We can also compute mean in one pass, then var and output in a second pass. That's two reads. PyTorch's native batch norm also does two passes in some cases. Since we are reading from global memory anyway, two passes is okay. But we can also use Welford's algorithm in one pass: compute mean and M2 in a single grid-stride loop, then compute var = M2 / count, then do a second pass for writing? Actually, we need the mean and inv_std before applying to each element. So unless we store the entire channel in shared memory (which might be too large, e.g., 125440 floats = 500KB > shared memory limit of 48KB), we need two passes. Two passes mean reading the input twice. That's still less than the reference: reference does conv (write), BN (read+write), relu (read+write). Total reads on the tensor: BN reads once, relu reads once = 2 reads. Our fused kernel reads twice and writes once. So same number of reads but one fewer write. Good. For eval mode, we only need one read and one write because we don't compute stats. So the kernel should be efficient enough. Now, for `fused_batch_norm_relu6_avgpool`, we can do: - For each channel c, compute mean and inv_std (training or eval). - Then grid-stride over N,H,W: compute z = clamp((x - mean)*inv_std*gamma+beta, 0, 6), accumulate sum into a per-N,C output. - This requires only one read of x! Because we compute stats and accumulate in the same pass. For training, we need stats first. So we either do a reduction pass first (read x) and then a second pass (read x again, compute & accumulate). Or if eval, we can do it in one pass. So eval: 1 read, 0 intermediate write. Training: 2 reads, 0 intermediate write. Reference: conv(write), BN(read+write), relu(read+write), pool(read+write output). That's 3 reads of the conv output, 3 writes. Our fused kernel: conv(write), then 2 reads and 1 write of a small vector. Massive win for training too. So writing the kernel for both train and eval is worthwhile. **Implementation strategy:** Write a C++ file with the following functions (bound via `load_inline`): - `at::Tensor fused_bn_relu6(at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps)` -> returns y. - `at::Tensor fused_bn(at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps)` -> returns y. - `at::Tensor fused_bn_relu6_avgpool(at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps)` -> returns y of shape (N, C). Actually, `nn.BatchNorm2d` parameters are named `weight` (gamma) and `bias` (beta). And `running_mean`, `running_var`. We can use `at::Tensor` for all. Since we know they are on CUDA and float32. Now, we need to write the CUDA kernels. Let's draft the CUDA code. For `fused_bn_relu6`: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void fused_bn_relu6_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ x, scalar_t* __restrict__ y, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gamma, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_mean, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_var, bool training, float eps, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int c = blockIdx.x; int count = N * H * W; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; if (training) { // Compute mean scalar_t sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < count; i += blockDim.x) { int n = i / (H * W); int hw = i % (H * W); int h = hw / W; int w = hw % W; int idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w; sum += x[idx]; } __shared__ scalar_t shared[256]; shared[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); // reduce for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { shared[threadIdx.x] += shared[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = shared[0] / count; __syncthreads(); // Compute var sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < count; i += blockDim.x) { int n = i / (H * W); int hw = i % (H * W); int h = hw / W; int w = hw % W; int idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w; scalar_t diff = x[idx] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } shared[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { shared[threadIdx.x] += shared[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var_val = shared[0] / count; } else { mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = rsqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < count; i += blockDim.x) { int n = i / (H * W); int hw = i % (H * W); int h = hw / W; int w = hw % W; int idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w; scalar_t v = (x[idx] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; v = v > 0 ? v : 0; v = v < 6 ? v : 6; y[idx] = v; } } ``` We need to handle blockDim.x not being exactly 256 (could be 128, etc.). Shared memory size should match max threads. We can allocate 512 floats. To be safe, we can use a template or dynamic shared memory. Note: The indexing is NCHW. In PyTorch, CUDA tensors are NCHW by default for conv. Wait, `x` is the output of conv2d, which is NCHW. So `x.size(0)=N`, `x.size(1)=C`, `x.size(2)=H`, `x.size(3)=W`. The kernel above has each block processing one channel. For small channels counts (e.g., 16), this means 16 blocks. For large (e.g., 960), 960 blocks. That's fine. Thread count per block: we can use 256 threads. That's a reasonable occupancy. However, there is a subtlety: For training, we do two passes over the data (mean and var). Each thread accesses memory with stride `blockDim.x`. The access pattern is not fully coalesced because threads in a warp access elements spaced by `blockDim.x` in the linearized array. Since `blockDim.x` is 256, adjacent threads in a warp (e.g., thread 0..31) access elements 0, 256, 512... which are far apart in memory (256 * 4 bytes = 1KB apart). This is bad for coalescing. Memory coalescing requires adjacent threads to access adjacent memory addresses. In NCHW, the innermost dimension is W. So for a given (n,c,h), the elements across w are contiguous. The best pattern is to have each thread handle a contiguous chunk, or to have the thread index correspond to the contiguous dimension. Better approach: flatten N*H*W and map thread index to linear index with stride `blockDim.x * gridDim.x`. But for coalescing, we want thread `i` in a block to access address `base + i`. However, here each block handles a different channel `c`, so the addresses are offset by `c*H*W`. If blockDim.x is e.g. 256, then threads 0..255 access addresses `base + 0..255` in the channel. That's perfectly coalesced! Wait, in my kernel, the loop variable is `i = threadIdx.x; i < count; i += blockDim.x`. So thread 0 accesses 0, 256, 512... This is strided across the entire N*H*W range, not contiguous. To make it coalesced, we should use `for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < count; i += blockDim.x)` but that is strided by blockDim.x. Actually, the block processes one channel. The elements of that channel are contiguous: base = n*C*H*W + c*H*W + h*W + w. Wait, the linear index is `((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w`. For a fixed `n,c,h`, `w` varies contiguously. But the total size is `N*H*W`. So linear index `i` can be decomposed into `n = i / (H*W)`, `rem = i % (H*W)`, `h = rem / W`, `w = rem % W`. If we have `threadIdx.x = t`, then address = `((n*C + c)*H + h)*W + w`. As `i` increments by `blockDim.x`, `n,h,w` change in a strided way. The memory layout is NCHW: all Ws for a given n,c,h are contiguous. So moving by `blockDim.x` in `i` jumps around in memory. To improve coalescing, we should keep the `w` dimension contiguous for threads. One approach: - Iterate over `n` and `h` in outer loops. - For each `n,h`, we handle `W` elements. Have each thread handle a contiguous slice of `W`? But `W` might be up to 112, which is less than block size (256). So we can assign each thread to a unique `(n,h,w)`. But then we need to map `threadIdx.x` to a linear index in a coalesced way. Better: `int total = N * H * W; int idx_base = blockIdx.x * total + threadIdx.x;` No. Wait, each block handles one channel `c`. The total elements in that channel is `N * H * W`. Let's flatten as `(n, h, w)`. The memory index is `offset + n*C*H*W + c*H*W + h*W + w`. Since `C*H*W` is the stride between adjacent n for the same c,h,w. But within a fixed n and c, memory is contiguous in h*W? Actually, `h*W + w` is contiguous in the channel. The stride between adjacent `h` is `W`. So the channel memory is just `N` slices of `H*W` contiguous floats. If we want coalesced access, we should have thread `t` process element `t` of the channel, then `t + blockDim.x`, etc. But `t` and `t+1` are adjacent in memory (if `t` is within the same n,h slice). However, if `blockDim.x` is 256, and `H*W` is e.g. 56*56=3136, then `t` from 0..255 are all in the first `n=0` slice (since 3136 > 256). So addresses are contiguous: `base + 0, base + 1, ... base + 255`. This is coalesced! Wait, is that true? Let's compute the linear index for `i = threadIdx.x` for a fixed channel `c`. The base address for the channel's first element is `base = c * H * W`. But there are `N` batches. In NCHW layout, the elements are ordered: for n=0, all c,h,w; then n=1, all c,h,w, etc. Actually, the stride for n is `C*H*W`. So the elements for a fixed `c` across different `n` are separated by `C*H*W`, not contiguous. Wait, NCHW: The dimensions are ordered N, C, H, W. So the linear index of `(n,c,h,w)` is `((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w`. For fixed `c`, as `n` increases, the index jumps by `C*H*W`. So the channel `c` elements are NOT contiguous across batches! They are interleaved with other channels. Specifically, for n=0, c=0, the next element in memory is n=0, c=0, h=0, w=1 (if W>1). After finishing w and h for c=0, the next element is n=0, c=1, h=0, w=0. So channel 0's elements are scattered: each pixel is separated by `C` channels from the next pixel in the same spatial location in the next batch? No. Let's unroll: For n=0, c=0, h=0, w=0..W-1 are contiguous. Then n=0, c=0, h=1, w=0..W-1 are contiguous after that. So the entire slice for (n=0, c=0) is contiguous (H*W floats). Then after that comes (n=0, c=1, h=0, w=0). So the channel slice for a fixed (n,c) is contiguous. But across n, it's not contiguous: (n=0,c=0) occupies bytes [0, H*W*4). Then (n=1,c=0) is at offset `C*H*W*4`. So the elements of channel `c` across all `n` are spaced by `C*H*W` elements. So if a block handles one channel `c` across all `n`, then the memory access pattern for `i = threadIdx.x; i < N*H*W; i += blockDim.x` is: - decompose i into (n, rem) where n = i / (H*W), rem = i % (H*W). - address = n*C*H*W + c*H*W + rem. - As `i` increments by `blockDim.x`, `n` may or may not change. - If `blockDim.x` divides `H*W`, then after some steps, `n` increments and the address jumps by `C*H*W - (blockDim.x - H*W)`. This access pattern is strided and not fully coalesced. However, because `H*W` is often a multiple of powers of 2 (e.g., 12544 = 112*112, or 784 = 28*28), and blockDim.x is 256, the pattern may be okay but not optimal. A better coalescing pattern: assign each warp to a contiguous chunk of `rem` (the within-channel index). For example, for a given `n` and `c`, the `H*W` elements are contiguous. We can have `blockDim.x` threads process elements `rem = threadIdx.x + blockDim.x * k` for `k` such that `rem < H*W`, and loop over `n`. That is: ``` for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < H*W; i += blockDim.x) { int h = i / W; int w = i % W; int idx = ((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w; // ... } } ``` In this case, for a fixed `n`, threads access `i = threadIdx.x, threadIdx.x + blockDim.x, ...`. Since `i` is contiguous in memory (because `h*W + w` is contiguous), threads with adjacent `threadIdx.x` access adjacent memory. This is coalesced! Even if `blockDim.x` exceeds `H*W`, some threads are just idle for that n. This is much better. So we should restructure the loops to iterate `n` outer, and `i` inner over `H*W`. Let's refine the kernel: ```cuda int c = blockIdx.x; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; int spatial = H * W; if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { sum += x[base + i]; } } __shared__ scalar_t sdata[256]; sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); // tree reduction for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = sdata[0] / (N * spatial); __syncthreads(); sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = x[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); // reduce for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var_val = sdata[0] / (N * spatial); } else { mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = rsqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t v = (x[base + i] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; v = v > 0 ? v : 0; v = v < 6 ? v : 6; y[base + i] = v; } } ``` Wait, `base + i` needs to account for h and w: `base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W`. Then `i` from 0 to H*W-1. `x[base + i]` is correct because the innermost dimension W is contiguous and H*W is contiguous. Yes! This is perfectly coalesced. This is much better. For the `fused_bn` kernel (no ReLU6), it's the same but without the clamp. For `fused_bn_relu6_avgpool`, we change the output: ```cuda __global__ void fused_bn_relu6_avgpool_kernel(...) { int c = blockIdx.x; int spatial = H * W; scalar_t mean_val, var_val; // compute same as above // ... scalar_t inv_std = rsqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; scalar_t sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t v = (x[base + i] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; v = v > 0 ? v : 0; v = v < 6 ? v : 6; sum += v; } // reduce sum within block __shared__ scalar_t sdata[256]; sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x/2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { out[n * C + c] = sdata[0] / spatial; } } } ``` But in this kernel, each block handles one channel `c`, but loops over `n`. For each `n`, we compute the sum and write to `out[n*C + c]`. Since `blockDim.x` threads cooperate to reduce, we need a `__syncthreads()` inside the `n` loop, which is fine. One issue: if `blockDim.x` is larger than `spatial`, many threads are idle in the `i` loop. That's fine. We should choose `blockDim.x` as the minimum power of 2 >= spatial? Or just fix it at e.g. 256. For spatial=1 (after pooling), 256 threads is overkill but okay. For spatial=12544, 256 threads will loop 49 times. That's fine. We need to make sure block reduction works even if `blockDim.x` is not a power of 2? We should set it to a power of 2, e.g., 256. To make the code robust, we can template the block size or just use 256 and dynamic shared memory. Let's fix block size at 256 for simplicity. We also need to handle fp32 only (since output must match fp32). So we can write kernels for float only. Now, for the C++ wrapper functions: ```cpp at::Tensor fused_bn_relu6( at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps) { auto y = at::empty_like(x); int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int threads = 256; int blocks = C; AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(x.scalar_type(), "fused_bn_relu6", [&] { fused_bn_relu6_kernel<scalar_t><<<blocks, threads, threads * sizeof(scalar_t)>>>( x.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), y.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_mean.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_var.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), training, static_cast<scalar_t>(eps), N, C, H, W ); }); return y; } ``` Wait, shared memory size: we need `threads * sizeof(scalar_t)` bytes. For float, `256 * 4 = 1024` bytes. That's tiny. We can also allocate static shared array of size 512 to be safe. But dynamic is fine. Actually, we might need to do two reductions per block (mean and var). We can reuse the same shared memory array. In the kernel, we declared `__shared__ scalar_t sdata[256];`. But if we want to be flexible with block size, we can do: ```cuda extern __shared__ float sdata[]; ``` But using templates with dynamic shared memory is a bit tricky because we need to declare `extern __shared__ scalar_t sdata[];`. That works if we pass the size in bytes in the launch config. However, `extern __shared__` with template type might not be allowed in some CUDA versions? It is allowed but the array name must be known at compile time? Actually, `extern __shared__ T sdata[];` is allowed. But for multiple kernels with same name, it's fine. Let's use `extern __shared__ char smem[];` and cast to scalar_t. Or simpler: just define a fixed-size shared array of e.g. 512. ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void kernel(...) { __shared__ scalar_t sdata[512]; // use sdata[threadIdx.x] } ``` As long as thread count is <= 512, it's fine. We'll set threads to 256. So `__shared__ scalar_t sdata[256];` is perfect. But if we want to be generic, we can define `256` as a constant. Wait, we might want to vary threads per block based on spatial size for avgpool? Not necessary. But there is a catch: `blockDim.x` might be less than 256 if we decide to launch fewer threads. No. One more thing: The reference code uses `nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)`. In our kernel, we write to a new tensor `y`. That's okay since we just need the output. The reference's inplace modifications are internal and don't affect the output of the block because the next operation reads the modified tensor. Our kernel writes to a new tensor, which is functionally equivalent. But we need to be careful: `inplace=True` in the reference means that during forward, the BN output tensor is modified in place by ReLU6. However, the BN module returns a new tensor (it doesn't modify in place unless we set `inplace=True` on BN, but BN doesn't have inplace). So `ReLU6(inplace=True)` modifies the tensor returned by BN in place. Since that tensor is not used elsewhere before ReLU6, the output is the same as applying ReLU6 out-of-place. So our approach is fine. Now, for the Python side. We'll define `ModelNew.__init__` to exactly replicate the reference's module construction. Then we define `forward` to manually walk through the layers. Since `self.features` is a `nn.Sequential`, we can access layers by index. We need to map the architecture. To avoid manually indexing with magic numbers, we can parse the `self.features` in `forward`. But since the architecture is static, we can just hardcode the indices or iterate. Let's iterate over `self.features`: ```python def forward(self, x): training = self.training eps = 1e-5 # default # features[0]: Conv2d(3,32,3,2,1) # features[1]: BN(32) # features[2]: ReLU6 x = F.conv2d(x, self.features[0].weight, None, self.features[0].stride, self.features[0].padding, self.features[0].dilation, self.features[0].groups) x = fused_bn_relu6(x, self.features[1].weight, self.features[1].bias, self.features[1].running_mean, self.features[1].running_var, training, self.features[1].eps) # Then for each block from index 3 to len(self.features)-4 (since last 4 are final conv, bn, relu6, pool) block_idx = 3 for t, c, n, s in inverted_residual_setting: for i in range(n): block = self.features[block_idx] # block is nn.Sequential if t != 1 and i == 0: # Wait, t is the expand ratio for the block. The same t applies to all repeats in the block. # The first block in the group may have stride s, others stride 1. pass block_idx += 1 # ... ``` But we need to know the structure of each block to apply the right fusion. Since t and stride vary per block group, we need to know for each block whether it has expand_ratio != 1. The architecture setting is: ```python inverted_residual_setting = [ [1, 16, 1, 1], [6, 24, 2, 2], [6, 32, 3, 2], [6, 64, 4, 2], [6, 96, 3, 1], [6, 160, 3, 2], [6, 320, 1, 1], ] ``` For each `(t, c, n, s)`, there are `n` blocks. All blocks in the group have expand_ratio `t`. The first block has stride `s`, others stride 1. So we can loop over this setting. Let's map block index `b` starting from 0 (corresponding to `self.features[3]`, `self.features[4]`, ...). Block 0: t=1, c=16, n=1, s=1. Since t=1, no expand. Block has 5 layers: DW conv, BN, ReLU6, PW conv, BN. Block 1: t=6, c=24, n=2, s=2. First block (i=0): expand, DW s=2, PW. Second block (i=1): expand, DW s=1, PW. Block 2: t=6, c=32, n=3, s=2. First: expand, DW s=2, PW. Others: expand, DW s=1, PW. Block 3: t=6, c=64, n=4, s=2. Block 4: t=6, c=96, n=3, s=1. Block 5: t=6, c=160, n=3, s=2. Block 6: t=6, c=320, n=1, s=1. Total blocks: 1+2+3+4+3+3+1 = 17. Yes. So in `self.features`, indices 3..19 are the 17 blocks. Index 20 is Conv2d(320,1280,1x1). Index 21 is BN(1280). Index 22 is ReLU6. Index 23 is AdaptiveAvgPool2d. Let's verify: features starts with [Conv, BN, ReLU6] (indices 0,1,2). Then 17 blocks (indices 3..19). Then final Conv (20), BN(21), ReLU6(22), Pool(23). Total 24 modules. So we can write the forward as: ```python x = F.conv2d(x, self.features[0].weight, None, self.features[0].stride, self.features[0].padding, self.features[0].dilation, self.features[0].groups) x = fused_bn_relu6(x, self.features[1].weight, self.features[1].bias, self.features[1].running_mean, self.features[1].running_var, training, self.features[1].eps) idx = 3 for t, c, n, s in inverted_residual_setting: for i in range(n): block = self.features[idx] if t != 1: # expand conv x = F.conv2d(x, block[0].weight, None, block[0].stride, block[0].padding, block[0].dilation, block[0].groups) x = fused_bn_relu6(x, block[1].weight, block[1].bias, block[1].running_mean, block[1].running_var, training, block[1].eps) # DW conv is block[3] if t!=1? Let's check. # block layers: [conv1, bn1, relu1, conv_dw, bn_dw, relu_dw, conv_p, bn_p] dw_idx = 3 pw_idx = 6 bn_dw_idx = 4 bn_p_idx = 7 else: # block layers: [conv_dw, bn_dw, relu_dw, conv_p, bn_p] dw_idx = 0 pw_idx = 3 bn_dw_idx = 1 bn_p_idx = 4 x = F.conv2d(x, block[dw_idx].weight, None, block[dw_idx].stride, block[dw_idx].padding, block[dw_idx].dilation, block[dw_idx].groups) x = fused_bn_relu6(x, block[bn_dw_idx].weight, block[bn_dw_idx].bias, block[bn_dw_idx].running_mean, block[bn_dw_idx].running_var, training, block[bn_dw_idx].eps) x = F.conv2d(x, block[pw_idx].weight, None, block[pw_idx].stride, block[pw_idx].padding, block[pw_idx].dilation, block[pw_idx].groups) x = fused_bn(x, block[bn_p_idx].weight, block[bn_p_idx].bias, block[bn_p_idx].running_mean, block[bn_p_idx].running_var, training, block[bn_p_idx].eps) idx += 1 # Final conv x = F.conv2d(x, self.features[20].weight, None, self.features[20].stride, self.features[20].padding, self.features[20].dilation, self.features[20].groups) x = fused_bn_relu6_avgpool(x, self.features[21].weight, self.features[21].bias, self.features[21].running_mean, self.features[21].running_var, training, self.features[21].eps) # x is now (N, 1280) x = self.classifier(x) return x ``` Wait, after pool, we flatten. Our avgpool kernel outputs (N, C). So `x` is already 2D. Then `self.classifier` is a Sequential of Dropout(0.0) and Linear. Dropout with p=0.0 is a no-op (identity). Linear is `nn.Linear(1280, num_classes)`. We can just call `self.classifier(x)` or `F.linear(x, self.classifier[1].weight, self.classifier[1].bias)`. Using the module is fine. This looks correct. Now, we need to write the C++ code and bind it. We need to be careful with `load_inline`. The C++ source should contain the CUDA kernels and the wrapper functions. We list the wrapper function names in `functions=[...]`. Since the C++ source string will be large, we should build it as a Python triple-quoted string. Important: Do not include `PYBIND11_MODULE`. `load_inline` will generate one. Also, we need to include <torch/extension.h>. Let's draft the C++ code. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> #define BLOCK_SIZE 256 template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void fused_bn_relu6_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gamma, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_mean, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_var, bool training, scalar_t eps, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int c = blockIdx.x; int spatial = H * W; int count = N * spatial; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; __shared__ scalar_t sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]; if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { sum += input[base + i]; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); // tree reduction for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = sdata[0] / count; __syncthreads(); sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = input[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var_val = sdata[0] / count; } else { mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = rsqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t v = (input[base + i] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; v = v > 0 ? v : 0; v = v < 6 ? v : 6; output[base + i] = v; } } } template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void fused_bn_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gamma, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_mean, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_var, bool training, scalar_t eps, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int c = blockIdx.x; int spatial = H * W; int count = N * spatial; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; __shared__ scalar_t sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]; if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { sum += input[base + i]; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = sdata[0] / count; __syncthreads(); sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = input[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var_val = sdata[0] / count; } else { mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = rsqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t v = (input[base + i] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; output[base + i] = v; } } } template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void fused_bn_relu6_avgpool_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gamma, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_mean, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_var, bool training, scalar_t eps, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int c = blockIdx.x; int spatial = H * W; int count = N * spatial; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; __shared__ scalar_t sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]; if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { sum += input[base + i]; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = sdata[0] / count; __syncthreads(); sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = input[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var_val = sdata[0] / count; } else { mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = rsqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; scalar_t sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t v = (input[base + i] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; v = v > 0 ? v : 0; v = v < 6 ? v : 6; sum += v; } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { output[n * C + c] = sdata[0] / spatial; } } } at::Tensor fused_bn_relu6_cuda( at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps) { auto y = at::empty_like(x); int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(x.scalar_type(), "fused_bn_relu6", [&] { fused_bn_relu6_kernel<scalar_t><<<C, BLOCK_SIZE>>>( x.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), y.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_mean.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_var.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), training, static_cast<scalar_t>(eps), N, C, H, W); }); return y; } at::Tensor fused_bn_cuda( at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps) { auto y = at::empty_like(x); int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(x.scalar_type(), "fused_bn", [&] { fused_bn_kernel<scalar_t><<<C, BLOCK_SIZE>>>( x.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), y.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_mean.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_var.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), training, static_cast<scalar_t>(eps), N, C, H, W); }); return y; } at::Tensor fused_bn_relu6_avgpool_cuda( at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps) { int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); auto y = at::empty({N, C}, x.options()); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(x.scalar_type(), "fused_bn_relu6_avgpool", [&] { fused_bn_relu6_avgpool_kernel<scalar_t><<<C, BLOCK_SIZE>>>( x.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), y.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_mean.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), running_var.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), training, static_cast<scalar_t>(eps), N, C, H, W); }); return y; } ``` Wait, the function names in C++ should match exactly what we put in `functions=[...]`. Now, some issues with the kernel: 1. `BLOCK_SIZE` is defined as 256, but we launch with `BLOCK_SIZE` threads. The shared array `sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]` is fine. 2. However, if `blockDim.x` is `BLOCK_SIZE`, the reduction loop `for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1)` works only if blockDim.x is a power of 2. 256 is a power of 2. Good. 3. But what if `spatial` is 0? Not possible. 4. One catch: `rsqrt` is a CUDA intrinsic. For `double`, it's `rsqrt`. For `float`, it's `rsqrtf`. But `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES` includes both float and double. We can use `1.0 / sqrt(var_val + eps)` to be generic, or use `rsqrt` which works for float and double? In CUDA, `rsqrt(float)` returns float, `rsqrt(double)` returns double? Actually, there is `rsqrtf` and `rsqrt`. Using `rsqrt` with scalar_t template might pick the right overload if we include `<cmath>`? In CUDA, `rsqrt` is a built-in for float and double. But for template, using `1.0 / sqrt(var_val + eps)` is safer and the compiler will optimize it. However, sqrt is slower. Let's just use `rsqrt(var_val + eps)` and rely on CUDA overloaded `rsqrt` for both `float` and `double`. Actually, for double it's `rsqrt` in CUDA? Yes, `__rsqrt` for float and `rsqrt` for double? Let's check: CUDA math API has `rsqrt(float)` and `rsqrt(double)`. Actually, I think `rsqrt(double)` is not standard; it's `1.0/sqrt(double)`. In CUDA C++, there is `rsqrtf(float)` and `rsqrt(double)`? I recall `rsqrt` is overloaded in CUDA for float, double, and half. To be safe, I'll use `1.0 / sqrt(var_val + eps)`. The compiler might not fuse it into rsqrt automatically? Modern nvcc does optimize `1.0/sqrt(x)` into `rsqrt(x)`. But to be safe, we can cast: ```cpp scalar_t inv_std = static_cast<scalar_t>(1.0) / sqrt(var_val + eps); ``` But if `scalar_t` is `float`, `sqrt` returns `float` (there is overload). If `double`, returns `double`. So this is safe. 5. Memory access: `input[base + i]` with `i` up to `spatial-1` and threads 0..255. If `spatial` < 256, only first `spatial` threads do work, and the rest are idle in the `i` loop but participate in reduction? Actually, if `spatial` < blockDim.x, the loop `for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x)` only executes for `i < spatial`. For `threadIdx.x >= spatial`, the body is skipped, so `sum` remains 0. That's fine for reduction. However, in the second phase (writing output), same thing: some threads are idle. 6. For `fused_bn_relu6_avgpool`, after reduction, only thread 0 writes output. We need `__syncthreads()` before moving to the next `n` iteration? Actually, inside the `n` loop, after reading input and accumulating `sum`, we do `sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads();` and reduce. Then thread 0 writes to `output[n*C + c]`. Then we start next `n`. But note: `sdata` is reused. After thread 0 writes, the next iteration starts with `sum = 0`. That's okay. However, we must ensure all threads have finished reading `sdata` before we overwrite it in the next iteration. The `__syncthreads()` before the reduction ensures all threads have written `sum`. The reduction has `__syncthreads()` inside. After the reduction, thread 0 writes. Then the loop iterates. Is there a `__syncthreads()` at the end of the loop? Not explicitly, but since thread 0 writes to global memory and other threads proceed to set `sum=0`, there is no race because the next iteration's first step is writing `sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum`. But we need to make sure thread 0's write to output is visible? It's fine; threads in a block are implicitly synchronized at the start of the next `__syncthreads()`, but there is none at loop boundary. Actually, after the last `__syncthreads()` in the reduction (when s=1), thread 0 reads `sdata[0]` and writes to global memory. Other threads may immediately proceed to the next iteration of the `n` loop and write to `sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum` (which is 0). This could happen before thread 0 reads `sdata[0]`! Wait, in the reduction loop: ```cpp for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` When `s = 1`, threads 0 and 1 do `sdata[0] += sdata[1]`, then `__syncthreads()`. After this `__syncthreads()`, thread 0 reads `sdata[0]` and writes to `output`. But there is no `__syncthreads()` after that. So threads 1..255 could move to the next `n` and do `sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum;` before thread 0 reads `sdata[0]`. Wait, thread 0 also reads `sdata[0]` after the `__syncthreads()`. After the loop ends, the code immediately does `if (threadIdx.x == 0) { output[n * C + c] = sdata[0] / spatial; }`. Thread 0 will execute this after the last `__syncthreads()`. But there is no guarantee about the order of execution of thread 0 vs other threads after the `__syncthreads()`. Other threads can advance immediately. So yes, they could overwrite `sdata[1]` before thread 0 reads `sdata[0]`? No, thread 0 reads `sdata[0]`, not `sdata[1]`. But other threads writing to `sdata[threadIdx.x]` where `threadIdx.x > 0` doesn't affect `sdata[0]`. However, what if `blockDim.x` is 256, and the next iteration, thread 0 writes `sdata[0] = sum` for the new `n`. Could thread 0's write to `sdata[0]` happen before thread 0 (previous iteration) reads `sdata[0]`? No, because thread 0 executes sequentially. It does the reduction, hits the last `__syncthreads()`, then executes the `if (threadIdx.x == 0)` block. Then it increments `n` and goes to the next iteration, where it writes `sdata[0] = sum`. So `sdata[0]` is safe. What about other threads? They might write to `sdata[threadIdx.x]` for the next `n` *after* the last `__syncthreads()` but *before* thread 0 finishes reading `sdata[0]` for the previous `n`. But they write to other indices, not index 0. So `sdata[0]` is not overwritten by them. So it's safe. Actually, there is a subtlety: `__syncthreads()` after the reduction ensures all threads have completed the reduction. After that, thread 0 reads `sdata[0]`. Since no other thread writes to `sdata[0]` in the next iteration until thread 0 itself does (because thread 0 writes to `sdata[0]` at the start of the next iteration's reduction), it's safe. So no extra `__syncthreads()` needed. But wait, what about the `sum = 0` initialization in the next iteration? It's a local variable. So fine. One more issue: `gamma`, `beta`, `running_mean`, `running_var` are 1D tensors of size C. Accessing them via `gamma[c]` is fine. They are on device. Now, we need to make sure that `x` is contiguous. The output of `F.conv2d` is contiguous. We can assume that. Another issue: The kernels use `blockIdx.x` for channel `c`. If `C` is large (up to 1280), that's 1280 blocks. For a GPU with many SMs, that's okay. But occupancy might be limited if each block uses a lot of registers or shared memory. Our kernel uses few registers and 256*4=1KB shared memory. So occupancy is high. However, there is a potential performance issue: For small `N*spatial`, the kernel is very light and many threads are idle. But this is okay. Let's think about numerical correctness. In training mode, we compute mean and var over the batch. The reference `nn.BatchNorm2d` computes: - `mean = input.mean([0, 2, 3])` - `var = input.var([0, 2, 3], unbiased=False)` (i.e., `var = sum((x-mean)^2) / (N*H*W)`). - Then `y = (x - mean[None, :, None, None]) / sqrt(var[None, :, None, None] + eps) * gamma[None, :, None, None] + beta[None, :, None, None]` In training, PyTorch also updates running stats, but since we don't call backward and don't need the updated running stats, we can ignore that. The reference forward in train mode returns the normalized batch output. So our kernel matches. For eval mode, PyTorch uses `running_mean` and `running_var` (not updated). Our kernel uses them. Good. ReLU6: `y = min(max(x, 0), 6)`. In PyTorch, `nn.ReLU6` computes `min(max(x, 0), 6)`. Our kernel does the same. For the avgpool: `nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1))` computes the mean over H and W dimensions. Our kernel does the same after BN+ReLU6. Potential precision differences: - Summation order: Our reduction sums across threads in a specific order. Due to floating-point associativity, this might differ slightly from PyTorch's implementation. PyTorch likely uses highly optimized reductions (e.g., pairwise or tree) on the GPU as well. But the difference should be small. With fp32 tolerance 1e-4, it should be okay for mean/var. However, if the variance is near 0, errors might be amplified. But for random inputs, variance is not near 0. But there is a subtle point: In the training path of our kernel, `mean_val` is computed by summing all elements in the channel, then dividing by count. Since each thread does a local sum in its own order (outer `n`, inner `i`), and then block reduction sums thread partial sums. The final sum might differ from PyTorch's sum. But the tolerance is 1e-4 absolute. For values in [0,6] after ReLU, the mean is about maybe 1-3. Summation error for ~125k floats is at most ~125k * 6 * 1e-7 ≈ 0.075 relative to the sum? Wait, fp32 has 24 bits of mantissa, ~7 decimal digits. Adding 125k numbers of size ~1, the naive sum error is on the order of ~1e-3 to 1e-2? Actually, the worst-case error of summing N numbers is about N * epsilon * max_value. For N=125440, epsilon=1.2e-7, max=6, error ≈ 125440 * 1.2e-7 * 6 ≈ 0.09. Dividing by count gives mean error of 0.09 / 125440 ≈ 7e-7. So mean is accurate to within ~1e-6. Similarly for variance. Then the normalized output error is small. So we should be well within 1e-4. For eval mode, no reduction errors from mean/var because they are provided exactly. So correctness should be fine. Now, what about the `classifier`? The reference uses `nn.Dropout(0.0)` which is identity. Then `nn.Linear`. We can just call `self.classifier(x)`. That will call cuBLAS for the matmul. That's fast. Alternatively, we can call `F.linear(x, self.classifier[1].weight, self.classifier[1].bias)`. Same thing. Now, we need to assemble the Python file. It will contain: 1. The `cpp_sources` string. 2. `load_inline` call. 3. `ModelNew` class definition. Let's draft the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> const scalar_t* __restrict__ gamma, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_mean, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_var, bool training, scalar_t eps, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int c = blockIdx.x; int spatial = H * W; int count = N * spatial; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; __shared__ scalar_t sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]; if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = input[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = static_cast<scalar_t>(1.0) / sqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { output[base + i] = v; } } } template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void fused_bn_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gamma, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_mean, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_var, bool training, scalar_t eps, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int c = blockIdx.x; int spatial = H * W; int count = N * spatial; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; __shared__ scalar_t sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]; if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { sum += input[base + i]; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = sdata[0] / count; __syncthreads(); sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = input[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var_val = sdata[0] / count; } else { mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = static_cast<scalar_t>(1.0) / sqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t v = (input[base + i] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; output[base + i] = v; } } } template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void fused_bn_relu6_avgpool_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gamma, const scalar_t* __restrict__ beta, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_mean, const scalar_t* __restrict__ running_var, bool training, scalar_t eps, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int c = blockIdx.x; int spatial = H * W; int count = N * spatial; scalar_t mean_val = 0; scalar_t var_val = 0; __shared__ scalar_t sdata[BLOCK_SIZE]; if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { sum += input[base + i]; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = sdata[0] / count; __syncthreads(); sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = input[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var_val = sdata[0] / count; } else { mean_val = running_mean[c]; var_val = running_var[c]; } scalar_t inv_std = static_cast<scalar_t>(1.0) / sqrt(var_val + eps); scalar_t g = gamma[c]; scalar_t b = beta[c]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; scalar_t sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t v = (input[base + i] - mean_val) * inv_std * g + b; v = v > 0 ? v : 0; v = v < 6 ? v : 6; sum += v; } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { output[n * C + c] = sdata[0] / spatial; } } } at::Tensor fused_bn_relu6_cuda( at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, double eps) { auto y = at::empty_like(x); int N static_cast<scalar_t>(eps), N, C, H, W); }); return y; } at::Tensor fused_bn_cuda( at::Tensor x, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bias, at::Tensor running_mean, at::Tensor running_var, bool training, But before that, is there any possibility to speed up further by also doing the `fused_bn` (no relu) as a no-op when BN is identity? No, because weight and bias may be loaded from a state dict. Actually, the reference init sets BN to identity. But after loading a state dict, they could be anything. In general, we must compute BN.
