Then we would need to write output. But we avoid the BN kernel. However, cuDNN conv with bias is as fast as conv without bias. So if we could fuse BN into conv bias, we could skip `fused_bn` kernel entirely for the project PW layers. But doing this on-the-fly requires: - `fused_weight = conv.weight * (bn.weight.view(-1,1,1,1) / sqrt(bn.running_var.view(-1,1,1,1) + eps))` - `fused_bias = bn.bias - bn.weight * bn.running_mean / sqrt(running_var + eps)` This requires broadcasting and elementwise ops on the weight tensor. We can precompute these in `__init__` or during forward. If we precompute in `__init__`, we can modify `conv.weight` and add a `bias` parameter to the conv. But then the state dict keys change (conv would have bias). To keep state dict compatible, we could register the fused bias as a buffer or parameter with a different name, but then loading a reference state dict would not load it correctly. We could compute it in `forward` each time. For a 1x1 conv, weight shape is (oup, hidden_dim, 1, 1). Fusing BN into it requires multiplying each output channel's filter by the BN scale. That's `oup` multiplications. For depthwise conv, weight shape is (hidden_dim, 1, 3, 3). Fusing BN into it also requires `hidden_dim` multiplications. This is negligible overhead. Then we call `F.conv2d(x, fused_weight, fused_bias, ...)`. This eliminates the `fused_bn` kernel launch and memory traffic for the project PW layers. And for expand PW + DW + project PW, we save one kernel and one memory round-trip. That's a good optimization. Similarly, we can fuse BN into the expand PW and DW convs as well. But those are followed by ReLU6. So we would do `F.conv2d(x, fused_weight, fused_bias, ...)` and then a custom ReLU6 kernel (no BN). That saves the BN reduction kernel. But wait, if the model is in train mode, we cannot precompute the BN scale because it depends on batch statistics. So fusing into conv weight only works for eval mode. We can handle both: In eval mode, fuse BN into conv and just apply ReLU6 after. In train mode, use our custom BN kernels. This would speed up eval mode significantly. Since the model might be evaluated in eval mode, this is great. But the problem doesn't specify train or eval. We need to match the reference. If the reference is in train mode, we must use batch stats. If in eval, running stats. We can check `training` in `forward`. If `not training`, we can fuse BN into conv weights on the fly? Precomputing in `__init__` is tricky because the running stats might be loaded from a state dict after `__init__`? Actually, if the evaluator copies state dict from reference to our model, it does so after instantiation. So if we precompute in `__init__`, we'd be using the initial running stats (identity), and after loading state dict, our fused weights would be wrong. However, if we compute fused weights on-the-fly in `forward`, we use the current BN parameters. That's safe. But computing fused weights on-the-fly for every forward call requires launching a small kernel or doing it on CPU. For 1x1 conv weights, it's a simple elementwise multiply. We can do it with PyTorch operations: ```python bn_w = block[bn_idx].weight bn_b = block[bn_idx].bias rm = block[bn_idx].running_mean rv = block[bn_idx].running_var eps = block[bn_idx].eps std = torch.sqrt(rv + eps) scale = bn_w / std bias = bn_b - rm * scale fused_weight = conv.weight * scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) fused_bias = bias x = F.conv2d(x, fused_weight, fused_bias, ...) ``` But this creates temporary tensors `scale`, `fused_weight`. For small convs (e.g., 1x1 with 192->24), `fused_weight` is 192*24*1*1 = 4608 floats, negligible. For 3x3 depthwise, hidden_dim * 1 * 3 * 3 = e.g. 192*9 = 1728 floats. Very small. So the overhead is tiny. Then instead of calling `fused_bn`, we just call the conv with bias. This reduces memory traffic and a kernel launch. But for layers followed by ReLU6, we still need a ReLU6 kernel after conv. Wait, we can also fuse ReLU6 into the kernel! But we don't have a custom conv kernel. We could use PyTorch's `F.relu6` after conv. That's one kernel. But we can also write a custom elementwise ReLU6 kernel. Since the conv output is already in global memory, `F.relu6` will read and write it. That's fine. Wait, but if we fuse BN into conv, we save the BN reduction kernel. For eval mode, that's a huge saving because BN is a reduction, which is relatively expensive. For train mode, we cannot fuse BN into conv because it depends on batch stats. We have to compute stats. But if the model is in train mode, our custom BN kernel handles it. So we can optimize eval mode further. But what if the test runs in train mode? The problem says "Replace the forward computation". The reference code doesn't set eval mode. But many evaluation frameworks do. Let's hedge: implement both paths. We can add a helper function `fuse_conv_bn(conv, bn)` that returns fused weight and bias. Then in `forward`, if not training: ```python if not training: # fuse scale = bn.weight / torch.sqrt(bn.running_var + bn.eps) # for conv with groups: if conv.groups == 1: fused_w = conv.weight * scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) else: # depthwise: weight shape (C_out, 1, K, K) fused_w = conv.weight * scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1) fused_b = bn.bias - bn.running_mean * scale x = F.conv2d(x, fused_w, fused_b, conv.stride, conv.padding, conv.dilation, conv.groups) x = F.relu6(x) ``` Wait, for `groups=hidden_dim` (depthwise), conv.weight shape is `(hidden_dim, 1, 3, 3)`. The output channels = hidden_dim. So `scale` has shape `(hidden_dim,)`. We can broadcast as `scale.view(-1, 1, 1, 1)`. So same. But we must be careful: `conv.weight` is a parameter. Modifying it in place would change the model state across calls. We should not modify `conv.weight`. Instead, compute `fused_w = conv.weight * scale.view(-1,1,1,1)`. Since `scale` is a tensor, this returns a new tensor. That's fine. However, doing this for every layer means we have many small elementwise multiplications. But these are very fast. For the first conv and final conv, same thing. In training mode, we cannot fuse. So we call conv without bias and then custom BN kernel. Let's think: is it worth adding this complexity? The custom BN kernel is also fast for eval mode because in eval mode there are no reductions. It just does `y = (x - mean) * scale * gamma + beta`. That's an elementwise kernel. Our custom kernel does exactly that. The only difference is that we can avoid calling it by using conv bias. But our custom kernel is already one kernel that does BN+ReLU6. So for eval mode, it's an elementwise kernel. Fusing into conv bias saves writing and reading the conv output for the BN step. Actually, no: If we use conv bias, cuDNN conv computes `output = conv(x, W) + b` internally, without writing an intermediate. So we save one write and one read compared to conv (write) + BN (read+write). That's a bandwidth saving. For small convs, bandwidth might be the bottleneck. But does PyTorch's `F.conv2d` with bias allocate the output and launch a cuDNN conv that fuses bias? Yes, cuDNN supports B (bias) in the convolution descriptor, so it's typically fused. So using `F.conv2d(..., bias=fused_bias)` is efficient. So for eval mode, fusing BN into conv bias is beneficial. For train mode, we cannot. But we can still write a generic path for both. However, writing conditional logic for every layer makes `forward` longer. But it's manageable. But wait! The reference model uses `nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)`. In eval mode, if we fuse BN into conv and then call `F.relu6(x)`, that will read and write the conv output. The reference would do BN (read+write) then ReLU6 (read+write). Our fused conv+bias does conv (write), then ReLU6 (read+write). We save one read and one write. Good. In train mode, our custom `fused_bn_relu6` does read+write. The reference does BN (read+write) + ReLU6 (read+write). So our custom kernel saves one read and one write. Good. So for train mode, the custom kernel is already a win. For eval mode, fusing BN into conv bias gives an extra win. But implementing eval fusion requires handling per-layer parameters. It can be done. Actually, there's a much simpler way: we can keep the custom `fused_bn_relu6` kernel for all cases, including eval. It already handles eval mode efficiently (no reduction, just elementwise). The memory traffic is conv(write) + fused(read+write). Fusing into conv bias would be conv(write+bias) + relu(read+write). So fused_bn_relu6 saves nothing compared to conv+bias+relu? Wait: - conv+bias: cuDNN writes output O (with bias added). - ReLU6: reads O, writes O. Total: 1 write (conv), 1 read+write (relu) = 2 writes, 1 read of O. - conv (no bias): writes O. - fused_bn_relu6: reads O, writes O. Total: 2 writes, 1 read of O. Same memory traffic! But conv+bias might be slightly faster because cuDNN fuses bias into conv. So conv+bias+relu might be better than conv+no_bias + fused_bn_relu6. reasoning
The compile failed because the host wrapper functions were not declared/defined in a header visible to the generated pybind11 code. With `load_inline`, the `functions=[...]` list references functions that must be declared/defined in the `cpp_sources` portion, not just the `cuda_sources` portion. I need to put the host C++ wrappers in `cpp_sources` and keep the kernels in `cuda_sources`, or put everything in `cpp_sources` with `__global__` qualifiers (but then it won't be CUDA). Actually, a simpler approach: I can just use the existing PyTorch operations but write a single large custom kernel to handle the entire forward pass. That might be too complex. Alternatively, I can put the host wrapper declarations in `cpp_sources` and the actual definitions in `cuda_sources`. Let me restructure the code properly. Or even simpler: I can write a single custom CUDA kernel that fuses each layer sequence. But making a single kernel for the whole MobileNetV2 is very complex. Instead, let me use a more structured approach with `cpp_sources` containing declarations and `cuda_sources` containing the actual CUDA code. Let me rewrite with proper separation.

Looking at my kernel more carefully: ```c if (training) { scalar_t sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { sum += input[base + i]; } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean_val = sdata[0] / count; __syncthreads(); ``` Wait, I see `mean_val = sdata[0] / count;` but `sdata[0]` is a shared memory value, and this line is executed by ALL threads in the block. But in C++ / CUDA, `sdata[0]` is in shared memory, and all threads read it after the `__syncthreads()`. This is fine. But what about after computing mean_val? In the variance computation: ```c sum = 0; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { int base = ((n * C + c) * H) * W; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < spatial; i += blockDim.x) { scalar_t diff = input[base + i] - mean_val; sum += diff * diff; } } ``` Each thread computes its local sum of squared differences. Then we reduce to get total sum, divide by count to get variance. This should match PyTorch's population variance. Wait, but there's an important subtlety: `mean_val` was computed using a sum that included ALL elements (including those processed by all threads). Then each thread computes `diff = input[base + i] - mean_val` where `mean_val` is the exact mean. This is mathematically correct for population variance. But wait, I just want to check: does `at::batch_norm` update running stats when `training=True`? Yes, it does if `running_mean` and `running_var` are provided. In my case, I pass them as non-null Tensor references. So `at::batch_norm` will update them in-place during training. My Python forward doesn't need to do anything extra. This means if the correctness test runs in training mode, my C++ wrapper will update the running stats just like the reference does. And since both use the same `at::batch_norm` implementation, the outputs should match exactly. If the test runs in eval mode, both use running stats and should match exactly. This approach should guarantee correctness! But will it be faster? Well, it eliminates the intermediate memory allocations between bn and relu6, and between relu6 and avgpool in the final layer. And it reduces Python overhead slightly. But the real test is whether it's faster than the reference. For more speedup, after getting correctness with this approach, I could try to optimize the convolutions or intermediate data movement. But first things first. But the problem explicitly asks to use `load_inline`. Hmm, another thought: maybe I should write a simple element-wise CUDA kernel that applies the BN affine transform + ReLU6, where the precomputed mean and inv_std are passed as parameters. In Python, I can compute these using PyTorch's exact ops. Let me think about this: ```python # Python if self.training: mean = x.mean([0, 2, 3], keepdim=True) var = x.var([0, 2, 3], unbiased=False, keepdim=True) # update running stats... else: mean = running_mean.view(1, -1, 1, 1) var = running_var.view(1, -1, 1, 1) inv_std = 1.0 / torch.sqrt(var + eps) affine_w = weight.view(1, -1, 1, 1) * inv_std affine_b = bias.view(1, -1, 1, 1) - mean * inv_std * weight.view(1, -1, 1, 1) # Call custom CUDA kernel: y = (x * affine_w + affine_b).clamp(0, 6) ``` In this approach: 1. Statistics computation uses PyTorch's exact implementation 2. The affine transform + relu6 is done in a custom element-wise CUDA kernel 3. No intermediate allocations 4. Should match PyTorch exactly This is much better! The only potential difference would be in the element-wise computation order, but since each element is computed independently and identically, there should be no difference. Let me write this version. Actually, I still need to handle the running stats update during training. In PyTorch, `nn.BatchNorm2d` updates `running_mean` and `running_var` during training. If I compute `mean` and `var` in Python but don't update the running stats, they will diverge from the reference after multiple training-mode calls. For correctness checks: - If eval mode: running stats don't change, no issue. - If training mode, but only called once per model instance: running stats don't matter for output since batch stats are used. But the reference model will have updated stats after the call. This doesn't affect the output of that call, but future calls would differ. To be thorough though, I should update running stats in Python when training=True. Looking at PyTorch's formula: ```python # PyTorch update (simplified) running_mean = (1 - momentum) * running_mean + momentum * mean.squeeze() running_var = (1 - momentum) * running_var + momentum * var.squeeze() * n / (n - 1) ``` Wait, PyTorch computes the biased variance (divides by n) during forward, but updates running_var with the unbiased variance estimate (divides by n-1), scaled appropriately. Actually, looking at PyTorch code more carefully: For training, the forward uses `var_biased = ((x - mean) ** 2).mean()`. The running_var is updated with: `running_var = momentum * var_biased * n / (n - 1) + (1 - momentum) * running_var` Wait, actually I think PyTorch uses the biased variance for the forward pass and stores the running variance as an exponential moving average of the biased variance, NOT corrected for Bessel's correction. Let me verify by looking at the source... Actually, in PyTorch's `batch_norm_cpu` and CUDA implementations, during training: ```cpp auto save_mean = input.mean(dim); auto save_var = input.var(dim, false); // false = population variance ... running_mean = momentum * save_mean + (1 - momentum) * running_mean; running_var = momentum * save_var + (1 - momentum) * running_var; ``` Wait, it uses `var(dim, false)` which is population variance. But `var(dim, correction=0)` in newer PyTorch. The running_var is the EMA of population variance. There's no `n / (n-1)` correction. But let me double check because I've seen conflicting info. Actually, I think for `nn.BatchNorm2d`, PyTorch computes the population variance during training and updates running_var with an EMA of that. But there's a subtlety: `nn.BatchNorm2d` uses `momentum` differently. The update is: `running_mean = (1 - momentum) * running_mean + momentum * mean` Wait, some sources say it's `(1 - momentum) * running_mean + momentum * mean`. Others might say `momentum * running_mean + (1 - momentum) * mean`. It depends on convention. Actually, PyTorch's convention is: ```python running_mean = (1 - momentum) * running_mean + momentum * mean running_var = (1 - momentum) * running_var + momentum * var ``` where `var` is the population variance. Wait no, looking at the actual PyTorch source code for BatchNorm2d: ```cpp // From aten/src/ATen/native/Normalization.cpp if (running_mean.defined()) { running_mean = at::addcmul(running_mean, momentum, save_mean - running_mean); running_var = at::addcmul(running_var, momentum, save_var - running_var); } ``` Actually no, that's not the exact source. Let me search my memory... In PyTorch 2.x, the update is done via `torch.lerp`: ```cpp running_mean.lerp_(save_mean, momentum); running_var.lerp_(save_var, momentum); ``` Where `lerp(a, b, t) = a + t * (b - a) = (1 - t) * a + t * b`. So: `running_mean = (1 - momentum) * running_mean + momentum * save_mean` This matches what I thought. And `save_var` is the population variance (computed with `unbiased=False`). But there's another subtlety: some versions of PyTorch might compute `save_var` with Bessel's correction. Let me check by looking at the `native_batch_norm` implementation. Actually, I found the exact code in PyTorch 2.0: ```cpp std::tuple<Tensor, Tensor, Tensor, Tensor> batch_norm_cpu_transform_input_template( const Tensor& input, const Tensor& weight, const Tensor& bias, const Tensor& running_mean, const Tensor& running_var, bool train, double eps) { // ... // compute mean and variance auto mean = at::mean(input, /*dim=*/{0, 2, 3}, /*keepdim=*/true); auto var = at::var(input, /*dim=*/{0, 2, 3}, /*unbiased=*/false, /*keepdim=*/true); // ... if (running_mean.defined()) { running_mean.mul_(1 - momentum).add_(mean.squeeze(), momentum); running_var.mul_(1 - momentum).add_(var.squeeze(), momentum); } } ``` Wait, I don't know the exact syntax. But traditionally, PyTorch BatchNorm2d uses population variance (not unbiased) for the forward pass and EMA update. So `var = x.var([0, 2, 3], unbiased=False)` is correct. And the update is: ```python running_mean = (1 - momentum) * running_mean + momentum * mean running_var = (1 - momentum) * running_var + momentum * var ``` With `momentum = 0.1` by default. OK, so if I replicate this exactly in Python, my running stats update will match the reference. But again, for correctness, if the test is in eval mode, none of this matters. If in training mode with single call, only the output matters, and I can compute the exact same mean/var using PyTorch's at::batch_norm or using my own computation with `x.mean` and `x.var`.
